study has taken place at the Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical University

==PAGE:6==
IV
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
The aim of the thesis is to develop an eﬃcient model for numerical limit state analysis of
reinforced concrete beams. It is assumed that the material is rigid-plastic and the lower
bound method is used. So far limit state analysis for beams has been done either with
conservative assumptions for the yield criterion on section force levels or with a detailed
modelling of the stresses along the entire beam. In this thesis the beam-model is developed
to optimise the section force distribution, and in check points along the beam a sub-model
is used to model and optimise the stress distribution in the cross section within the yield
criterion.
Finite element formulation is used and both the beam and the cross section is build up
by elements.
The elements in the cross section are deﬁned as bars, representing thin
walls. Distributed reinforcement in two transverse directions of the cross section walls is
assumed. With this assumption a yield condition for reinforced concrete disk can be used.
Two programs are developed in the thesis. BeamOpt which optimises the load carrying
capacity of an entire beam or frame structure and SubOpt which optimises the stress
distribution in a speciﬁc cross section in isolation. The programs are in the thesis used
for various analysis. In the analysis numerical results from the developed programs has
been compared to analytical results, experimental results and numerical results from a
disk-program.
As the developed beam-model does not model all the stresses in the beam, but only in
check points along the beam, considerations has been whether the model provides true
lower bound solutions. The concern is whether the variation in the stresses between two
check points are statically admissible. From the various analysis and an extension of the
sub-model - to account for point equilibrium in the stresses in a small piece of the beam
- it is assessed that as long as there are not two critical cross sections close to each other,
the stresses has freedom enough to vary in a way that is statically admissible between the
critical sections. Thus for most realistic beam cases, the developed beam-model provides
a safe lower bound solution for the load carrying capacity.

==PAGE:8==
VI
==PAGE:9==
Resumé
Formålet med dette speciale er at udvikle en eﬀektiv model til numerisk brudgrænseanal-
yse af armerede betonbjælker.
Materialet antages at være stift-plastisk og nedreværdi
metoden benyttes. Indtil nu har brudgrænseanalyser for bjælker været lavet med enten
konservative antagelser for ﬂydebetingelsen på snitkraft niveau eller med detaljeret mod-
ellering af spændingerne langs hele bjælken. I dette speciale optimerer bjælkemodellen
på fordelingen af snitkræfterne langs bjælken og i speciﬁkke kontrolpunkter benyttes en
submodel til at modellere og optimere spændingsfordelingen i et tværsnit indenfor ﬂyde-
betingelsen.
Elementmetode formulering er benyttet og både bjælken og tværsnittet er delt op i el-
ementer. Elementerne i tværsnittet er deﬁneret som stænger, der repræsenterer tynde
vægge i tværsnittet. Det antages, at der er jævnt fordelt armering i to retninger i disse
vægge og en ﬂydebetingelse for armerede betonskiver kan benyttes.
Der er i specialet udviklet to programmer. BeamOpt, som optimerer bæreevnen af hele
bjælken eller rammekonstruktionen og SubOpt, som optimerer spændingsfordelingen i et
speciﬁkt tværsnit isoleret. Programmerne er i specialet benyttet til forskellige analyser.
I analyserne sammenlignes de nummeriske resultater fra programmerne med analytiske
resultater, eksperimentielle resultater og numeriske resultater fra et skiveprogram.
Da bjælkemodellen udviklet i specialet ikke modellerer alle spændingerne i bjælken, men
kun i kontrolpunkter langs bjælken, har der været overvejelser om hvorvidt modellen
giver ægte nedreværdiløsninger. Spørgsmålet er om variationen i spændingerne mellem to
kontrolpunkter er statisk tilladelige. Fra de forskellige analyser og en udvidelse af sub-
modellen - så den kan medtage punktligevægt i spændingerne i et lille stykke af bjælken
- er det vurderet at så længe der ikke er to kritiske snit i bjælken tæt på hinanden, har
spændingerne frihed nok til at variere mellem de kritiske snit i bjælken på en måde, der er
statisk tilladelig. For de ﬂeste realistiske bjælketilfælde, vil bjælkemodellen give en rigtig
nedreværdiløsning for bæreevnen.

==PAGE:16==
==PAGE:17==
1
Introduction
For calculations of the load carrying capacity of reinforced concrete structures, it is com-
mon practice to use plasticity theory for describing the material behaviour. For perfect
plastic material, there are many diﬀerent ways in which the stresses can be distributed in
the material. Diﬀerent numerical limit state analysis are developed to optimise upon the
distribution of stresses and forces in diﬀerent kind of structures, based on perfect plasticity
assumption. For reinforced concrete beams this has so far been done either with conserva-
tive assumptions for the yield criterion on section force levels or with a detailed modelling
of all the stresses along the beam. For the last method, a model has been developed by
Kasper Paaske Larsen as described in his Ph.D. Thesis [Larsen, 2010].
The model in [Larsen, 2010] uses solid elements along the whole beam where one element
represents a zone in the cross section. The rectangular zones can be deﬁned by the user,
but their geometry are ﬁxed along the whole beam. As modelling the stresses along the
whole beam can be relatively heavy computational wise, it is of interest to see if a model
can be developed, which uses the section force level along the beam, and only the stress
level in a chosen number of sections along the beam.
In models and calculations of load carrying capacities of beams, assumptions are often
made on how the cross section carries the stresses. E.g. when both shear force and mo-
ment force are present in a cross section, assumptions can be made that the moment is
only taken by the ﬂanges of the cross section (lower and upper part) and the shear force
by the web (center part). This assumption will in many cases lead to an almost optimal
load carrying capacity, but it can often be more optimal to also let the web obtain some
of the moment, if the moment is relatively large compared to the shear force. What the
most optimal stress distribution in a speciﬁc cross section is depends on the materials and
geometry of the cross section, and the combination of the acting section forces the speciﬁc
cross section. In the zone model in [Larsen, 2010] the zones are ﬁxed along the beam, and
the distribution of the stresses in the zones is to some extend rigid. It is therefore of inter-
est to model the cross section in a way that allows for a more ﬂexible stress distribution
in the cross section.
The aim of this thesis is to develop a numerical program to optimise the rigid plastic
load carrying capacity of reinforced concrete beams, where the design parameters along
the beam are the section forces, and a sub-model is used in a number of sections along
the beam to model the stresses in the cross section. The sub-model optimises the stress
distribution, in each cross section that is checked, within a yield condition on stress level.
Thus a model is developed which can optimise the section force distribution along the
beam and the stress distribution in the critical cross sections.
1
==PAGE:18==
Introduction
A lower bound method is used and, as the stresses are only modelled in individual sections
along the beam, an interesting question is whether the variation in the stresses between
two check points in the beam is statical admissible, thus if the model provides a real lower
bound solution. This circumstance is investigated and discussed throughout the report.
Besides modelling a whole beam or a frame structure the sub-model is also developed, so
it can be used in isolation. This means that a cross section with speciﬁc section forces
can be modelled without modelling the whole beam. In this analysis the section forces in
the cross section are the applied load, and thus the load that is maximised. In this way
it can be checked how large section forces can be applied at a cross section for a speciﬁc
combination of section forces.
In the extend of this thesis, the cross section is modelled only with thin-walled elements.
Thus plane stresses can be assumed for the elements for which the yield condition for
reinforced concrete disks from [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010] can be used. The shape of the
cross section can be designed by the user from these thin elements. A further development
of the program could be to make it applicable to e.g. classic rectangular cross sections
where the longitudinal reinforcement is not evenly distributed, but concentrated in areas
like the top and bottom of the cross section.
The practical use of this limit state analysis can be for diﬀerent kinds of beam constella-
tions. It can be used when designing frame structures or large beams like the pylons in a
bridge, where the load carrying capacity is optimised upon in order to save some material.
Another application can be to show if existing beam structures, which might be originally
designed with the use of elasticity theory, can obtain more load than originally designed
for.
1.1
Structure of the report
First Chapter 2 contains some theory behind some of the assumptions used in the thesis.
Some theory for rigid plastic material and its application for reinforced concrete are pre-
sented. Furthermore a plane stress state in a point is set up and the equilibrium equations
for such a point presented, as this point equilibrium is object of discussion throughout the
report.
Chapter 3 contains a thoroughly description of the model used for the limit state analysis.
It describes the optimisation problem, the beam-model, the sub-model and the connection
between them.
Chapter 4 describes the implementation of the model in the program, and the program
structure.
Furthermore some numerical test are illustrated to make sure the model is
implemented correctly.
In Chapter 5 various results are found by use of the developed program. They are analysed
and compared with analytical solutions, with experimental results and with results from
a numerical disk program. First results from the sub-model in isolation are analysed and
afterwards analysis with the whole beam-model are provided.
Chapter 6 contains a discussion of some of the results and assumptions behind the model.
It also provides some ideas of further extensions and work with the model.
At the end Chapter 7 provides a conclusion of what is achieved and found in this thesis.
2
==PAGE:19==
2
Theory
For the limit state analysis a rigid-plastic material is assumed and the load carrying ca-
pacity is determined using the lower bound method. This chapter provides some theory
behind the material assumption and use of method. As reinforced concrete is the main
material used in the thesis, its behaviour in connection to the rigid-plastic assumption
is outlined. The yield condition used for the reinforced concrete in the model is a yield
condition developed for reinforced concrete disks and this will also be presented. Further-
more equilibrium conditions for generalised plane stresses are set up, as the use of this
equilibrium is discussed throughout the thesis.
2.1
Rigid-plastic material
A rigid-plastic material is a material that undergoes no deformation until the yield cri-
terion is reached. When the yield criterion is reached the material can obtain any size
of deformation without any change in the stresses. For an uni-axial case the stress-strain
relationship is as seen on Figure 2.1. The deformations are pure plastic which, in contrary
to elastic deformations, are permanent.
ε
σ
fY
-fY
Figure 2.1: Uni-axial stress-strain relation for rigid-plastic material.
For a general stress state a set of generalised stresses Q1, Q2, ..., Qn is deﬁned. The point
of yielding can then be deﬁned by a yield condition, [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010]:
3
==PAGE:20==
Theory
f(Q1, Q2, ..., Qn) = 0
(2.1)
Combinations of Q1, Q2, ...Qn that fulﬁls (2.1) can give arbitrarily amount of strains with-
out any changes in the stresses. If f ≤0, the stress state is within the yield condition
and the material is not yielding, which for a rigid-plastic material means, that there are
no strains. f ≥0 is not a possible stress state.
When the stress state is on the yield surface, the directions of the strains q1, q2, ..., qn, can
be determined by von Mises’s ﬂow rule, [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010]:
qi = λ ∂f
∂Qi
(2.2)
where λ is a scalar indicating that the size of the strains is arbitrary. The strain state
found by the ﬂow rule is perpendicular to the yield surface.
2.1.1
Reinforced concrete
As the tensile strength of concrete is small compared to its compression strength, the
idea of reinforcement is to strengthen the concrete in areas exposed to tension.
The
reinforcement is not designed to carry transverse or shear force, but is assumed only to
carry uni-axial stresses. In Figure 2.2 the stress-strain relation for concrete exposed to
compression, and for steel is shown together with the rigid-plastic assumption represented
by the blue lines.
The concrete has a linear-elastic behaviour at the beginning, and when internal micro-
cracking begins the behaviour becomes non-linear. At the top, where fc is found, the
micro-cracking turns into discrete cracks and due to developing of cracks, the curve is
softening. The rigid-plastic assumption looks like a rough estimation.
The curve for the steel is a typical stress-strain curve for mild steel. It has a linear elastic
behaviour all the way until the yield stress is reached. After that hardening occurs and
large deformations are possible without loosing strength. It is thus seen that steel, with
its very ductile behaviour, suits the rigid-plastic assumption better than the concrete.
ε
σ
νfc
fc
(a) Concrete
ε
σ
fy
(b) Steel
Figure 2.2: Stress-strain curves
4
==PAGE:21==
2.1 Rigid-plastic material
It is in Figure 2.2a seen that there is a eﬀectiveness factor ν in front of the compression
strength for concrete. The compression strength fc, which corresponds to the top point
of the concrete working curve, is found from uni-axial tests. The strength of the concrete
has been shown to be reduced for other load situations due to diﬀerent crack formations.
Many studies have been made on which value, that ν should obtain in diﬀerent situations.
Some of the studies are seen in [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010]. The eﬀectiveness factor is not
the focus in the thesis and it will be set to 1 in the examples. For real designs though, the
value should be chosen with care.
Even though concrete is not a rigid-plastic material, it has been shown that the rigid-plastic
assumption in many cases is good at predicting the load carrying capacity of reinforced
concrete structures, [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010]. It is not that good at predicting the plastic
deformations, but - as the scope of this thesis concerns only the limiting load, and the
deformations are not considered - the rigid-plastic assumption seems suitable.
2.1.2
Extremum principles
For rigid-plastic materials the lower bound and upper bound methods can be used to
determine a limit load, also called collapse load, for the structure. The extremum principles
behind the methods are outlined in the following. These are proved in [Nielsen and Hoang,
2010].
Lower bound theorem
The lower bound theorem states that for a statically admissible distribution of the stresses,
where all stress points are within or on the yield surface, then the corresponding load is
below or equal to the actual collapse load.
The lower bound method based on the lower bound theorem, strives to ﬁnding a statically
admissible stress distribution that maximises the load and thereby gets closer to the actual
collapse load. The advantage of the lower bound method is that the collapse load which
is found is on the safe side.
Finding the maximum lower bound load is an optimisation problem that ﬁts well in pro-
gramming formats.
Upper bound theorem
The upper bound theorem states that for a kinematically admissible collapse mechanism,
ie. a possible strain ﬁeld, the upper bound collapse load can be found by setting internal
work and external work equal to each other.
Upper bound methods strives at ﬁnding a collapse mechanism that corresponds to the
smallest possible collapse load. The disadvantage of the upper bound method is that the
collapse load is not on the safe side.
5
==PAGE:22==
Theory
Uniqueness theorem
According to the uniqueness theorem a load which fulﬁls both the lower bound and upper
bound theorem is the true and uniquely deﬁned collapse load.
This theorem can be used in programming problems, where the primal problem is optimi-
sation of the limiting load by use of the lower bound method. The solution to the primal
problem is then also the solution to the dual problem (upper bound), and thereby the
collapse mechanism corresponding to the collapse load can also be found.
In this thesis the lower bound method is used. A program is developed which maximises
the load in the model based on the lower bound method. The optimisation algorithm
that is used also ﬁnds the solution to the dual problem, but this solution, ie. the collapse
mechanism, is not analysed in the thesis.
2.1.3
Yield condition for reinforced concrete disks
The cross sections in this thesis are implemented as plane walls with distributed rein-
forcement in two perpendicular directions as seen in ﬁgure 2.3. As seen in the ﬁgure the
planes are deﬁned in the (x, s)-coordinate system. x is the longitudinal direction of the
beams in local coordinates and the geometries of the cross sections are deﬁned in the local
(y, z)-coordinates. s is thereby a coordinate that follows the plane of the cross section
transverse to the x-direction.
The planes can be regarded as reinforced concrete disks, and the yield condition for re-
inforced concrete disks in [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010] is used. This is a yield condition
developed by using rigid-plastic theory. The assumptions behind are brieﬂy described in
the following.
For the concrete, the modiﬁed Coulomb criterion is used, assuming zero tensile strength. It
is assumed that reinforcement can only carry longitudinal tensile and compressive stresses.
The reinforcement bars are further assumed to be placed in small enough intervals, so the
forces in the bars can be regarded as equivalent stresses which are evenly distributed in
the cross section area.
These assumptions do not ﬁt the reality in details, but they are necessary to avoid getting
an analysis that is too complex. However it turns out that these details are not essential
when the purpose is to determine the load carrying capacity, [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010].
6
==PAGE:23==
2.1 Rigid-plastic material

Figure 2.3: Reinforced concrete disk
To ﬁnd the tensile and compression strength of the composite material the reinforcement
degree is used, deﬁned as:
Φx = Asxfy

tfc

where Asx and Ass are the reinforcement area per unit length in the x- and s-directions

The tensile strength of the composite

fts = Φsfc

and the compression strength as:
fcx = (1 + Φx)fc ,
fcs = (1 + Φs)fc
(2.5)
It is noted that as Φx and Φs tend to be small compared to 1, and as compression strength
in the reinforcement bars requires enough support against buckling from e.g. perpendicular
reinforcement, it is further assumed that the compression strength of the reinforcement is
zero leading to:
fcx = fcs = fc
(2.6)
Using the described assumptions and parameters, the yield condition for an orthotropic
disk is derived in [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010] as:

3
Problem and model formulation
As described in the introduction, the aim of the thesis is to develop a beam-model to be
used for a numerical limit analysis of a reinforced concrete beam, assuming rigid plastic
material. The purpose of the model is optimising the distribution of the section forces
along the beam, and using a sub-model at speciﬁc check points in the beam to optimise the
stress distribution in those cross sections. The sub-model should ensure that the stresses
are kept within the yield criterion. Besides connecting the sub-model to the beam-model,
the sub-model is also developed in a way so it can work without the beam-model. This
means that a speciﬁc cross section with known section forces can be optimised upon
without modelling the entire beam. This is referred to as sub-model optimisation.
The idea behind the beam-model is to make a program which, due to eﬃciency, does not
need to know all stresses in every point of the beam, and furthermore a program that can
optimise the distribution of the stresses in a way so the critical cross sections can take
as high section forces as possible. Making a model in this way, the variation between the
stresses in two check points are not analysed. This can be a drawback of the model, as in
reality there are limits on the stress variation as seen from the equilibrium equations in
(2.8) and (2.9). The question can be asked whether the stresses in the beam are statically
admissible and the solution thus is a real lower bound solution, when not all stresses along
the beam is known. Or if the solution is close at being a real lower bound solution. These
issues will be discussed throughout the report.
Problems can occur with high stress variation across a point exposed to a high concentrated
load or support. It is therefore assumed that at such places a diaphragm is placed, which
can obtain the large stress variations. Therefore big stress variations across locations of
concentrated loads are not seen to be an issue in the model.
The aim of this chapter is to describe the set up of the beam-model, sub-model and the
optimisation method. It has been aim to give a good understanding of the assumptions
involved, the equations used and the formulation of the matrices. The way the model
and the matrices are implemented so they suit the optimisation algorithm is described
in Chapter 4.
In the process of developing the numerical model, various models and
assumptions has been considered, but mainly the ﬁnal model and assumptions are outlined
here.
11
==PAGE:28==
Problem and model formulation
3.1
Optimisation problem
The aim of the numerical analysis is to maximise the load a given beam can carry by
use of the lower bound method. This means that the maximum load which fulﬁls the
statically admissible stress ﬁeld should be found. As mentioned, the model developed in
this project might not provide the true lower bound solutions. This will be discussed later
in the report.
The generalised optimisation problem can be formulated as:
max
β
λ
s.t.:
H β = λ R + R0
f(βi) ≤0,
i = 1, 2, ..., m
(3.1)
where λ is the load factor which is the object to be maximised, β is a vector that contains
all the design variables, which on the beam level are section forces and on the cross
section level (sub-model) are stresses, H is the equilibrium matrix that makes sure all the
conditions and equilibrium in the models are satisﬁed, R is the scalable load vector, R0 is
the constant load vector, f(βi) ≤0 is the generalised yield condition and m is the number
of yield conditions in the model.
3.2
Second-order cone programming
The yield conditions that are used in this thesis are of second-order degree. In order to
model this numerically second-order cone programming (SOCP) is used.
For writing the program, Matlab is used as the programming tool, but the program
Mosek is used for the optimisation algorithm, as it is good for handling second-order
conic optimisation. Matlab and Mosek are integrated in such a way, so Mosek optimi-
sation algorithm can be called from Matlab script.
An alternative to the use of second-order cone programming could be to linearise the
second-order yield conditions. In that way linear programming could be used instead.
But with second-order programming the exact yield conditions can be implemented.
The second-order cone problem is in Mosek deﬁned in the form, [Mosek Manual, ]:
minx
cT x
s.t.
lc

≤

lx
≤

≤

x ∈Q
(3.2)
where:
c deﬁnes the objective variables
x is a vector with the design variables and the objective variables
A is the system matrix containing all the constraints

lc,uc are the lower and upper limits for the constraints
lx,ux are the lower and upper limits for the variables in x
Q is a Cartesian product of the convex cones.
For doing the optimisation with second-order cone programming the problem in (3.1)
should therefore be made to ﬁt (3.2).

formulations of the cones.
3.3
Beam-model
As mentioned, the aim of the global beam-model is to make an eﬃcient model, that does
not have to know the stresses in every point of the beam. On the beam level it is therefore
the distribution of the section forces that is optimised upon and the stresses are only
examined at speciﬁc points of the beam chosen as check points that should fulﬁl the yield
condition. The optimisation of the section force distribution is done in order to achieve
the highest possible load factor λ.
The yield condition is found by use of a sub-model, which is outlined in Section 3.4. In
this section only the beam-model with the section forces is described.
3.3.1
Description and assumptions
The beam elements are made as bars. The design variables for a beam element are the
section forces. Within the scope of this thesis the beam is only modelled in 2D. This
means that the section forces available are the normal force N, the shear force V , and the
moment force M. The direction of the shear force is in the z-direction and of the moment
force around the y-axis, as seen from Figure 3.1.

These distributions are chosen in order to model constant distributed transverse line load
at each beam element.

It is noted that at nodes in the beam where a large concentrated force is applied, a big
jump in the shear force appears. This results in big diﬀerences in the stresses at the two
sides of the load. In this model the section forces at each side of the node are checked
for yielding in the sub-model individually. Thus the sudden change in the stresses across
the node is not taken into account in the model. As mentioned, it is assumed that at
such points a diaphragm is inserted in the structure to handle the sudden change in the
stresses.


(3.15)
where n is the total number of elements and m the total number of nodes in the model. It
is thereby seen that Hb is build up with the equilibrium equations for all the nodes ﬁrst
and the elements second. Assembling of Hb is done as in standard ﬁnite element method,
placing the contributions from each element at the correct locations.
It is further noted that the supports on the beam are set in the same degrees of freedom
as the nodal loads. They are implemented in the model by removing the corresponding
rows in Hb and Rb. In this way the supports can obtain an arbitrary force. If wanting to
ﬁnd the reactions, the optimised section forces in βb can be multiplied with the original
Hb, and the resulting nodal load vector Rb then contains the reaction sizes.
3.3.3
Check points along the beam
As mentioned, the yield conditions are found by use of a sub-model which will be outlined
in the next section. It is ensured that the section forces are not higher than the sub-model
allows in each check point along the beam elements. The number of check points in each
beam element, nce, is set as an input in the model. The check points are evenly distributed
along the elements. If nce = 2 the check points are placed at both ends of the element. If
nce = 3, the third check point is placed at the center of the elements and so on. If only
having point loads, just two checks per element are necessary, as the moment distribution
in that case is linear between the nodes. If line load is present more check points are
needed, but the number of check points should be assessed in each case, as the maximum
moment is not necessarily at the center or the ends of the beam elements. The section
forces in each check point should be found as illustrated in Figure 3.5.

along the beam elements are found by use the the functions in (3.6)-(3.11) and multiplying
with M1, M2 and M3 respectively.

3.3.4
Beam-model optimisation
Along each beam element a number of check points are chosen. In each check point the
sub-model is used to model the stresses in the cross section and make sure they are within
the yield surface. The aim of the beam-model is to optimise the distribution of the section
forces, to give the largest possible applied load, within the yield conditions at each check
point. The problem formulation in (3.1) is for the beam-model speciﬁed to:
max
βb,βs
λ
s.t.:
Hb βb = λ Rb + Rb0
C1i βb + C2i γi = 0
D1i γi + Hsi βsi = 0
E1i βsi + E2i αi = 0
i = 1, 2, ..., nc
αi ∈Q
(3.19)
where nc is the total number of check points.
The vectors contain:
βb:
Design variables (section forces) for the beam-model
γi:
Section forces at check point i
βsi:
Design variables (stresses) for the sub-model at check point i
αi:
Auxiliary variables at check point i
The role of the matrices are:
19
==PAGE:36==
Problem and model formulation
Hb:
Ensure equilibrium in the beam-model
C1i,C2i:
Find the section forces in each check point along the beam
D1i,Hsi:
Ensures equilibrium in the sub-model
E1i,E2i:
Deﬁnes the auxiliary variables in the sub-model
3.4
Sub-model
The purpose of the sub-model is to optimise the stress distribution in the cross section at
each check point along the beam. The optimisation is done in order for the cross section
to be able to obtain the largest amount of section forces for each speciﬁc section force
combination. Besides being connected to the beam model as in (3.19), the sub-model is
also made in a way so it can be used without the beam model. This means that it should
be possible to look at just one cross section without modelling the whole beam. This can
be done by specifying the combination of section forces in the speciﬁc cross section, and
maximise this combination of section forces with a load factor. This optimisation problem
is formulated in Section 3.4.4.
When developing the sub-model, the inclusion of the equilibrium condition in a point from
(2.8) has been a concern. Not including it means that the solution might not be a real
lower bound, but including it requires an extended sub-model.
To begin with the sub-model is made without the point equilibrium. In Section 3.5 a
sub-model including the point equilibrium is developed to analyse if the point equilibrium
has any inﬂuence on the results. This sub-model is referred to as sub-model 2. In the
following the basic sub-model without the point equilibrium is outlined.
3.4.1
Description and assumptions
The cross section is located in the local (y, z)-plane. It is modelled in 3D as seen in Figure
3.6, meaning that both shear force in the y- and z-direction, moment around the y- and z-
axis and torsion can be taken into account. The reason is that if making an arbitrary cross
section in the (y, z)-plane, equilibrium between the stresses and section forces is necessary
even for the force directions that are zero, as some of them are in the 2D beam-model.
It should be noted that the sign convention of Vz and My in the sub-model are opposite as
for V and M in the beam-model due to their deﬁnition. This is taken care of when con-
necting the two models. These deﬁnitions might be done with better compliance between
the sub-model and beam-model.
20
==PAGE:37==
3.4 Sub-model
x
y
z
N
T
Vy
Vz
My
Mz
Figure 3.6: Section forces in cross section
The cross section is modelled with thin walls and it is build up by elements. The elements
are deﬁned between two nodes as seen in Figure 3.7a. The s-direction is deﬁned in the
direction of the elements. The arrow in the element shows the deﬁned positive direction
of the shear stresses. This direction is deﬁned as going from node 1 to node 2 in the local
node numbering.
As also seen from Section 2.2 and Figure 2.4 the walls in the cross section are deﬁned in
the (xs)-plane. As the cross section is thin-walled plane stress can be assumed meaning
no normal stresses transverse to the (xs)-plane. The normal stresses in the s-direction are
assessed to be insigniﬁcant and are therefore not taken into account in the model. This
is common practice in beam models. The stress parameters in the cross section are thus
normal stresses, σx, in the x-direction and shear stresses, τxs, in the (x, s)-plane. It is the
distribution of these two stresses that is optimised upon.
In Figure 3.7b the element is shown in the (xz)-plane in order to show the stress parameters
along the cross section. In each element there are one normal stress parameter and two
shear stress parameters. This is deﬁned in order to get a constant normal stress distribution
and a linear shear stress distribution in an element. The linear shear stress distribution
is necessary in order to get a continuously shear ﬂow along the cross section.
These
stress distributions are assumptions that are made to get a rather simple sub-model. This
requires though, that enough sub-model elements are used in the cross section, to get the
most optimal stress distribution. An alternative could be to use stress distributions of
higher degree in an element. This would require less elements in the cross section, but
on the other hand more yield condition checks at each cross section element. This is not
done in the thesis.

Conditions for the stresses
The point equilibrium in (2.8) is not used in this sub-model. The conditions for the stresses
are thereby:
• Shear continuity: Continuity in the shear ﬂow between the elements.
• Equivalence equations: Equivalence between the section forces and the stresses in
the cross section.
Shear continuity
The shear ﬂow H is deﬁned as shear force pr. unit width, thereby H = τxst, where t is
the thickness of the element.
Equivalence equations
The equivalence equations that needs to be fulﬁlled are seen below.
Z

σx(y, z)dA = N
(3.20)
Z

τxy(y, z)dA = Vy
(3.21)
Z

τxz(y, z)dA = Vz
(3.22)
Z

(3.23)
Z

(3.24)

σx(y, z) (−y)dA = Mz
(3.25)
22
==PAGE:39==
3.4 Sub-model
Equation (3.23) has been found in [Krenk and Høgsberg, ].
In the equivalence equations above τxy and τxz are used instead of τxs. The parameter
s represents the direction along the elements, as seen in Figure 3.7a. In the equivalence
equations above, e.g. τxy is used instead of τxs in (3.21). τxy is the stress, τxs, along the
element projected to the (xy)-plane. This notation is used as only shear stress in the (xy)-
plane contributes to shear force in the y-direction. Likewise τxz is a projection of τxs to the
(xz)-plane. It is further noted that there is a minus sign in (3.25). This is because a posi-
tive moment around the z-axis gives negative stresses for positive values of y and opposite.
3.4.2
Finite element formulation
To ensure that the conditions for the stresses are fulﬁlled - like continuity in the shear
ﬂow between the elements and the equivalence equations - the following relation for an
sub-model element is set up:
hsβs = rs
(3.26)
where the s in the subscript is used to indicate that it is the sub-model. hs is the equilib-
rium matrix for an element, βs contains the stress parameters, and rs contains the section
forces.
In this section of the thesis an element means a sub-model element, unless else is stated.
Shear ﬂow
To ensure continuous shear ﬂow between the elements, equilibrium for the shear ﬂow at
each node needs to be set. In Figure 3.8 a case with two elements is illustrated. It is
seen how the shear stresses τe1 contribute negative to equilibrium in the x-direction at
the nodes and τe2 contribute positive. To allow for diﬀerence in the thickness between
elements, it is the shear ﬂow teτe which is used for equilibrium and not just the stress,
where te is the thickness of element e. For the case in Figure 3.8 with two elements and
three nodes, the equilibrium equations for the shear ﬂow at each of the nodes would be:
Top node:
t2τ22 = 0
Middle node:
t1τ12 −t2τ21 = 0
Bottom node:
−t1τ11 = 0
(3.27)
Thus it is also seen that the shear stresses at the free edges become zero.

To satisfy the equivalence equations in (3.20)-(3.25) the contribution from each element

importance. The point (yg, zg) is the center of gravity for the whole cross section, which is
the reference point chosen for the section forces. dye and dze represents the distances from
the center of gravity of the whole cross section to the center of gravity of the element.

disregarded in the center of gravity. The dimensions are calculated by:

Global level
The contributions hs for each element are assembled in a global equilibrium matrix for
the sub-model Hs that fulﬁls:
Hsβs = Rs
(3.35)
The total number of rows in Hs are m + 6, where m is the number of nodes in the sub-
model and 6 is the number of section forces, which does not change no matter the number
of elements in the sub-model. The contributions from the shear continuity should enter
the relevant nodes and the contributions from the elements to the equivalence equations
are assembled in the bottom 6 rows. In this way the contribution from each element, e.g.
Ne, Vye, to the section forces sums up to the total section forces in the cross section. The
global βs and the load vector Rs (when looking at the sub-model in isolation) are build
as:

3.4.3
Yield conditions
In this thesis two diﬀerent yield conditions are modelled. Von Mises yield condition and
the yield condition for a reinforced concrete disk [Nielsen and Hoang, 2010]. Von Mises
was used as a start in the project as it is a bit more simple than the yield condition
for a concrete disk. Von Mises yield condition is used in the thesis for comparison with
analytical results and the implementation is therefore outlined here, even though the main
focus in the thesis is on concrete beams.
As the yield conditions are convex, and due to the distribution of the stresses in a sub-
model element, it is on the safe side to use the yield conditions at each end of each element
in the sub-model. This means that two stress points per element in the sub-model are
constrained to be within the yield surface.
Von Mises yield criteria
Von Mises yield condition is suitable for isotropic materials, like steel. Even though the
main focus in the thesis is on concrete beams, the program can just as well be used for
modelling steel beams.
Von Mises yield condition for plane stress can be formulated as, [Krenk and Høgsberg,
2011]:
q
σ2x + σ2s −σxσs + 3τ 2xs ≤fy
(3.38)
where fy is the yield strength. As σs is not included in this model, (3.38) is simpliﬁed to:
q
σ2x + 3τ 2xs ≤fy
(3.39)

3.4.4
Sub-model optimisation
The beam-model uses the sub-model for the the yield condition in each check point along
the beam. If it is wanted only to optimise the stress distribution in the cross section at
one point of the beam, without modelling the entire beam, the sub-model can be used by
itself. For pure sub-model optimisation, the optimisation problem looks like:
max
βs
λ
s.t.:
Hs βs = λ Rs
E1 βs + E2 α = 0
α ∈Q
(3.50)
where Rs contains the section forces, as seen in (3.36).
3.5
Sub-model including point equilibrium
In order to include the point equilibrium in (2.8), diﬀerent approaches has been inves-
tigated. One approach used only a single section in the beam, as is the case with the
sub-model described in the previous. It did not succeed though to use the method in a
way so it can be solved with the second order cone programming. Another approach is
to use two sections for each sub-model, i.e. look at a small piece of the beam. In the
following this approach is described and this sub-model is referred to as sub-model 2. The
basic sub-model described in the previous is referred to as sub-model 1.
3.5.1
Description and assumptions
An element now has a width dx in the x-direction. The stress distribution is still assumed
to be constant for normal stresses and linear for shear stresses. It is noted that the shear
stresses are also assumed to vary linearly in the x-direction in order to keep the model
rather simple. Still the normal stresses in the s-direction, σs, are excluded from the model.
31
==PAGE:48==
Problem and model formulation
Thereby the stress distribution in an element could be as seen in Figure 3.10a. The stress
parameters corresponding to such a distribution are seen in Figure 3.10b. Compared to
sub-model 1, there is here a double amount of stress parameters per element.
The two sides in an element - corresponding to the two section cuts in the beam - are
referred to as the left side and the right side. This is also seen on the index L and R on
the stress parameters. The element is deﬁned in the same way as for sub-model 1, seen
in Figure 3.7a. It is thus still thin-walled seen in the (y, z)-coordinates and deﬁned from
local node 1 to 2. The shear stress parameters are thus still deﬁned from the nodes, so
τLe1 and τRe1 are located at the local node 1 and τLe2 and τRe2 at the local node 2.
σx

(a) Stress distribution

(b) Stress parameters
Figure 3.10: Element in sub-model 2
Conditions for the stresses
Besides shear ﬂow continuity and equivalence between the stresses and the section forces
in each sections, the point equilibrium in (2.8) is now added. The conditions are thereby
summarised as follows:
• Shear continuity: Continuity in the shear ﬂow between the shear in the left side and
right side respectively.
• Equivalence equations: Equivalence between the section forces and the stresses.
Should be fulﬁlled at both sides.
• Point equilibrium: The stresses should fulﬁl horizontal equilibrium in the element.
Besides fulﬁlling shear continuity and the equivalence equations in both sides now, the
point equilibrium is the main diﬀerence from sub-model 1.
Equivalence equations
As sub-model 2 for a start is used to compare results with sub-model 1, it is chosen to
assume that only normal force N, shear in the z-direction Vz and moment around the
y-direction My can be present to keep it more simple. Thus all other section forces are set
32
==PAGE:49==
3.5 Sub-model including point equilibrium
to zero, as for the global beam model. In Figure 3.11 a section of the beam, dx long, is
illustrated. Here it can be seen that the section forces in sub-model 2 are deﬁned in the
same way as in the beam-model, as this was more convenient when looking at a piece of the
beam. Thus the shear force and moment force diﬀers in sign convention from sub-model
1. Furthermore it is seen, that a line load can be applied as well.

==PAGE:53==
4
Model implementation
In the previous chapter the beam-model and sub-model were outlined and described. The
optimisation problems for the beam-model and the individual sub-model are given in
(3.19) and (3.50) and the matrices for those optimisation problems have been found. In
this chapter one further step is taken and an overview of how the problems are implemented
in Matlab is given.
How the optimisation problems are set up to ﬁt into second-order cone programming
in (3.2) will be outlined, and an introduction to the program will be given in terms
of input and output parameters, and ﬂow diagrams.
The programs containing beam-
model optimisation and individual sub-model optimisation are called BeamOpt and SubOpt
respectively. Coding details will not be explained, but all the Matlab functions in the
programs are seen in Appendix B.
At the end of the chapter some tests for the sub-model and beam-model are made to check
that the optimisation problems are implemented correctly.
4.1
SOCP setup
The optimisation problems in (3.19) and (3.50) should be set up in a way to ﬁt second-
order cone programming (3.2). This means that all the matrices are assembled in the
system matrix A, and the limits lc, uc, lx and ux and the cones are set.
4.1.1
Beam-model optimisation
For the beam-model optimisation in (3.19) the system matrix A is set up as seen below.
In the top the equilibrium for the beam-model is set up and then the sub-models for all
the check points are following in the diagonal. The connection between the beam-model
and sub-models are seen from the matrices C1,ei. It is noted that the subscript for a
check point i is here extended to ei as it here is of importance which element the check
point belongs to, to get the section forces from the right element. ne is the number of
beam elements and nc is the number of check points in a beam element. It is seen that
for element 1, C1,ei is placed to the left at the location multiplied with the section forces
for element 1, and for element ne, C1,ei is placed more to the right at the location that is
multiplied with the section forces from element ne.
37

How the sub-models As,ei are set up is seen below. Actually all the matrices As,ei in
the sub-model are the same no matter which beam element or check point is regarded, as
the beam-model in this thesis can only handle the same cross section in all elements. Of
course the model can be extended to make it possible to apply diﬀerent cross sections for
diﬀerent beam elements, and in that case As,ei should diﬀer for diﬀerent cross sections.

On top of the matrices seen above, the variables in x are illustrated at the relevant
locations. Below here the ’whole’ vector is shown with the order of the variables. As
seen all the section forces in the beam-model come ﬁrst, followed by the variables for the
sub-model at element 1 check point 1, element 1 check point 2 and so on. At the end, the
load factor λ is located. As seen in the large system matrix A it is multiplied by the load
vector Rb.
38
==PAGE:55==
4.1 SOCP setup
x = [βb γ11 βs,11 α11 ... γ1nc βs,1nc α1nc ...... γne1 βs,ne1 αne1 .. .γnenc βs,nenc αnenc λ]′
The vectors lc and uc are seen to be set as lower and upper limits for the equations formed
by Ax. In the model constant load Rb0 can be applied as seen in (3.19). Those values
are set as inputs in lc and uc at the relevant locations. All other inputs in lc and uc are
set to zero.
The vectors lx and ux are the limits for the variables in x. For the auxiliary variables, the
variables representing the strength of the material should be ﬁxed by setting the inputs
in both lx and ux to the strength, e.g. fy. For the last variable λ, the lower limit should
be set to 0 and the upper to inf, as it should be positive. All the other variables should
be free, and -inf and inf are used to specify this.
Cone set up in Mosek
The type of cone j is for Mosek deﬁned by:
cones{j}.type = ’MSK_CT_QUAD’
cones{j}.type = ’MSK_CT_RQUAD’
for quadratic cones and rotated quadratic cones respectively. The variables for the cones
are set by writing the number for the relevant variables in x like:
cones{j}.sub = [x1 x2.. xn]
where x1, x2...
xn correspond to x1, x2..xn in (3.3) and (3.4).
Optimisation in Mosek
The vector c is set to the same length as x and with the number 1 at the last entry, to
set the objective parameter in the optimisation to λ.
The optimisation algorithm in Mosek is called by the procedure mosekopt(’maximize’,prob),
where prob is a struct containing all the matrices, vectors and cones described in this sec-
tion.
4.1.2
Sub-model optimisation
For the individual sub-model optimisation in (3.50) the system matrix A has similarities
to As,ei, but here the section forces are set in the load vector Rs, as previously described.
39
==PAGE:56==
Model implementation
Hs
E1
E2
A  =
βs
α
‐Rs
λ
x is here just:
x = [βs α λ]
The lower and upper limits, c and the cones are set with the same principals as in the
beam optimisation described just above.
4.2
Program structure
As mentioned earlier, Mosek is used for the optimisation algorithm, but all the coding
is done in Matlab, as Mosek can be called from Matlab. The two programs BeamOpt
and SubOpt are made by creating a bunch of Matlab functions, all connected. In the
following each program are described by their input parameters, output parameters and
a ﬂow diagram for the Matlab functions.
The code is developed by the author, but inspiration is taken from similar programs by
Morten Andersen Herfelt and a beam program provided at the DTU course 11309 Analysis
of Frame Structures. Besides the structure of the program, inspiration has mainly been
used for creating the stress plots, using sparse and spalloc matrices, to set up the cones
and call Mosek correctly.
It has of course been the aim to make a good and eﬃcient code, but as this is not the
main focus in the thesis, the code can probably still be optimised upon.
4.2.1
BeamOpt program
In the beam-model optimisation two diﬀerent sub-models are implemented. One using
von Mises yield criteria and one using concrete yield criteria. Sub-model 2 with the point
equilibrium is not included in the beam-model, but only in SubOpt.
The two sub-models are implemented in the program, so an input parameter tells if it is
von Mises or reinforced concrete that is used. It has been the aim to make a program that
can implement a new sub-model or beam model without to much trouble.
BeamOpt input
For the beam-model optimisation element topology should be deﬁned for both the beam-
model and the sub-model. As some variables in the program are related to the beam-model
40
==PAGE:57==
4.2 Program structure
and some to the sub-model, it is chosen to make the struct beam and the struct sub to
easier distinguish between the variables.
In the extend of this thesis all beam-model elements obtain the same cross section. For
the sub-model though, the elements can obtain diﬀerent parameters, both with respect to
thickness, reinforcement degree and material parameters.
The input parameters are described in the following:
sub.model:
Deﬁnes if it is von Mises or concrete sub-model that is to be used.
sub.mat=’vm’ for Von Mises and sub.mat=’co’ for concrete.
beam.ncheck:
Sets number of check point for each beam element.
beam.X:
Deﬁnes the node coordinates for the beam.
beam.T:
Deﬁnes the element topology for the beam.
sub.X:
Deﬁnes the node coordinates for the cross section.
sub.T:
Deﬁnes the element topology for the cross section.
sub.Gi:
A cell with the material parameters for element type i.
sub.Gi.t:
Thickness of the element.
sub.Gi.fy:
Yield strength of the steel (for Von Mises sub-model).
sub.Gi.fcx:
Compression strength fcx (for concrete sub-model).
sub.Gi.fcs:
Compression strength fcs (for concrete sub-model).
sub.Gi.ftx:
Tension strength ftx (for concrete sub-model).
sub.Gi.fts:
Tension strength fts (for concrete sub-model).
sub.Gi.nufc:
Concrete compression strength with eﬀectiveness factor νfc (for
concrete sub-model).
beam.BC.Rvar.node:
Variable nodal load [node dof value].
beam.BC.R.node:
Constant nodal load [node dof value].
beam.BC.Rvar.line:
Variable line load [element value] (only one direction is possi-
ble, therefore dof is not deﬁned).
beam.BC.R.line:
Constant line load [element value].
beam.BC.V.node:
Supports [node dof].
BeamOpt output
The main output used in this thesis is res.sol.itr.xx, which is the vector x containing
all the design variables. It is seen in Section 4.1 what the order of the design variables
are. The last variable in res.sol.itr.xx is the load factor λ.
It is also of interest if the solution obtained with the optimisation algorithm is in fact
the optimal solution. This can be seen from rcodestr. If rcodestr =’MSK_RES_OK’, the
solution is ﬁne. If rcodestr =’MSK_RES_TRM_STALL’, the optimisation problem is badly
scaled.
The parameter MU - which is the diﬀerence between the primal objective dual
objective - is then a good indicator of how close to optimal the solution is. MU should be
close to zero for a good solution. Preferable below 10−8.
It is noted that Mosek also provides the solution to the dual problem. This can be found
from res.sol.itr.y. This is however not used in this thesis, as the deformation ﬁgures
are not investigated.

==PAGE:59==
4.2 Program structure
4.2.2
SubOpt program
The program SubOpt makes the individual sub-model optimisation in (3.50).
In this
program sub-model 2, with the point equilibrium, is also implemented. This means that
it contains sub-model 1, both with the von Mises and the reinforced concrete sub-model,
and sub-model 2, just with concrete yield condition.
SubOpt input
The input for SubOpt is mainly the same as for BeamOpt, but obviously the topology for
the beam should not be deﬁned. As sub-model 2 can also be used, a parameter is deﬁning
if sub-model 1 or sub-model 2 is used. This parameter is sub.model = 1 for sub-model 1
and sub.model = 2 for sub-model 2.
There are also some diﬀerences with respect to the load and supports. In the sub-model
optimisation the load input is the section forces. Thereby the nodal load and line load in
BeamOpt is not of relevance here. Furthermore supports can not be set in the sub-model.
They are reﬂected from the applied section forces.
The scalable section forces are for sub-model 1 set in the vector:
sub.load = [N Vy Vz T My Mz]
The program is made so it is also possible to insert constant section forces, which are not
scalable. This is deﬁned as:
sub.load0 = [N Vy Vz T My Mz]
When using sub-model 2 the section forces on the left side are deﬁned in the input. Care
should be taken of the sign convention of VL and ML, due to the deﬁnitions, as VL and
ML are deﬁned in opposite directions from Vz and My in sub-model 1. As sub-model 2
can only model 2D section forces, the other section forces should just be set to zero:
sub.load = [NL 0 VL 0 ML 0]
SubOpt output
The output for SubOpt is similar to the output for BeamOpt.
SubOpt ﬂowchart
The ﬂowchart for SubOpt, seen in Figure 4.2, is of course a bit more simple than for
BeamOpt. It is noted though, that there are now two diﬀerent buildhsel, as hs diﬀers
between sub-model 1 and sub-model 2. Furthermore probbuild is made without sparse
matrix, as the system matrix is not as large as for the beam-model optimisation. A spalloc
matrix is used in stead.

4.3
Stress plots
In the following the stress plots in the cross sections, found by plotSubStress, are brieﬂy
described, as the stress plots are used a lot during this report.
For beam-model optimisation, the sub-model is used in several check points along the
beams. When plotting the stresses it should therefore be chosen which check point that is
plotted. On Figure 4.3 a plot of the normal stress and a plot of the shear stress is shown.
In the top right corner it is written which section forces the cross section is exposed to. It
is done with the section force deﬁnition in the sub-model. The cross in the middle shows
the geometrical point of gravity for the cross section.
In Figure 4.3a, the normal stresses are plotted, which are quit straight forward to com-
prehend. The green shows positive stresses and the red shows negative stresses.
For the shear stress in Figure 4.3b the arrows show how the direction of the shear stress
is deﬁned in the topology (from local node 1 to local node 2). If the shear stress is green,
it means that the stress runs in the same direction as the arrow, if the shear stress is red
however, it means that the shear stress is negative and runs in the opposite direction than
the arrows.

4.4
Test of the models
During set up of the matrices and implementation of the model, diﬀerent errors can be
made. It is therefore important to test by simple examples if the implemented model acts
as expected and complies with the assumptions and constraints. First the sub-model is
tested, both with von Mises and concrete yield condition. Afterwards, the beam-model is
tested.
4.4.1
Von Mises sub-model
The purpose of the tests of the von Mises sub-model is both to check that the equivalence
between the section forces and stresses works as expected, that the yield condition is not
violated, but also to see that the yield condition is reached in the critical points, to make
sure the optimisation works. Furthermore diﬀerent geometries of the cross section will be
tested and it is checked if the continuity of the shear ﬂow works even for geometries like
I-proﬁle and double celled closed rectangle. Most of the tests concerning the geometries,
shear ﬂow and equivalence, are worked out with von Mises yield condition, as this is
isotropic material and therefore the results are more simple to comprehend, than for
concrete material.
Some of the tests are shown here in the report, but most of them are put in Appendix
A.2.1.
Yield surface
Before doing the sub-model tests, the von Mises yield surface is illustrated.
As only
two stress parameters in a material point, σx and τxs are used, the yield surface can be
illustrated in 2D, as seen in Figure 4.4. The red crosses on the yield surface in the ﬁgure are
45
==PAGE:62==
Model implementation
tests made on single stress points. The single stress point tests were worked out in order
to learn the use of Mosek optimisation algorithm and see if von Mises yield condition
were implemented correctly.
In short, the single point test is made by starting with the stress point [σx0 τxs0], and
setting up von Mises yield condition in the conic form (3.40), to ﬁt the Mosek second-
order conic optimisation algorithm in (3.2). The optimisation should then give the scalar
λ for which [σx,opt τxs,opt] = λ[σx0 τxs0], which, if done correctly, lies on the yield surface.
The red crosses in the ﬁgure are thereby [σx,opt τxs,opt] found for diﬀerent starting points.
As they lie on the yield surface, it is seen that the right procedure were used. For the
algorithm doing the single point test, see Appendix B.3.1.
Furthermore the single point test has been used in the test of the sub-models, to check
at some points if the stress state lies on the yield surface. In those cases a stress point
found by the sub-model has been copied to the starting point of the single point test and
if λ = 1, the point is on the yield surface.

+0.1m × 0.05m × 287.5MPa × 0.1m = 0.59MNm
which is correct and the shear stresses result in the shear force:

which is also correct. In the program the stresses in each end of each sub-model element
are constrained to be within the yield surface. This is also seen to be the case here:

4.5 Test of the beam-model
4.4.2
Concrete sub-model
As the equilibrium matrix for the reinforced concrete sub-model is the same as for the von
Mises sub-model, it is not necessary to check if there is equilibrium between the stresses
and section forces and continuity in the shear ﬂow, as this has been checked for von Mises.
Therefore it is for the concrete sub-model just checked if the stress states lie on or within
the yield surface.
Yield surface
As for von Mises yield condition, a single point test is done. Here for two diﬀerent material
cases as seen in Figure 4.11. The material parameters used for the tests are fy = 500 MPa,
fc = 40 MPa, ν = 1 and t = 0.2 m. The reinforcement areas are seen from the ﬁgures.
Beside showing that the optimised stress point lies on the yield surface, the tests also
shows how big diﬀerence that is on the yield surface for diﬀerent reinforcement degrees.

4.4.3
Sub-model 2
For sub-model 2 a case with four elements is tested. It has been calculated if point equi-

that the stresses at both sides were within the yield condition. From that it is assessed,
that sub-model 2 has been implemented correctly.

The four test have been tested both with von Mises yield condition and the concrete yield

Both test 3 and test 4 have been worked out, as an interesting diﬀerence between them
is seen for the concrete yield condition. With the rolled support in test 3, the normal
force is zero in the beam, but when both end are supported horizontally, a normal force in
compression along the beam becomes present. The reason is that compression increases
the shear capacity, and thereby allows for larger section forces. The section forces for these
two tests, can be seen in Appendix A.2.2.
From the various beam tests it is assessed that BeamOpt is implemented correctly. In the
result and analysis chapter, some of the analysis also shows that the program works as
supposed.
52
==PAGE:69==
5
Results and analysis
The developed programs BeamOpt and SubOpt are tested, and it is assessed that the models
are implemented correctly. In this chapter results are found by use of the programs. The
results are analysed by comparison to analytical results, experimental results and results
found by a disk program.
First SubOpt is used to analyse the optimisation of stress distributions in cross sections.
Von Mises sub-model is used to ﬁnd shear-moment curves and compare them to analytical
curves from [Vrouwenvelder, 2003], the concrete sub-model is used to investigate the op-
timal stress distributions with a box cross section as example and sub-model 2 is used to
compare results from the basic concrete sub-model to see if the point equilibrium makes
any diﬀerence.
After the sub-model analysis BeamOpt is used for numerical analysis of whole beams.
Results for a simple supported high beam is compared to analytical solutions with ho-
mogeneous stress ﬁelds, results for a continuous beam with a rectangular cross section
with concentrated longitudinal reinforcement, is compared to analytical and experimen-
tal results, and results for another continuous beam is compared to result from a disk
program.
5.1
Sub-model results with von Mises
In this section results for sub-model 1 with von Mises yield criteria are analysed. First a
convergence analysis is made in order to see how many element are needed for the results
to converge.
[Vrouwenvelder, 2003] has come up with analytical V M-curves (curves showing the inter-
action between shear force capacity and moment force capacity). Von Mises yield condition
is used for those curves, and cases both for rectangles and I-proﬁles are provided. Both
cases will be analysed numerically and compared with the analytical curves. Furthermore
the stress distribution for some cases will be shown.
A parameter denoting how much of the shear capacity that is used compared to the
moment capacity is deﬁned as:
rV M = V/Vp
M/Mp
(5.1)
53
==PAGE:70==
Results and analysis
where Vp is the pure shear plastic capacity and Mp is the pure moment plastic capacity.
It is noted that for all von Mises analysis in this section the yield strength fy = 300 MPa
is used.
5.1.1
Convergence analysis
The number of elements needed in the cross section in order to obtain convergence of the
results, depends on the cross section shape and the load case. The convergence analysis
is done with a thin rectangular cross section and a mix between shear force and moment
force which gives relatively large variations in the stresses along the cross section. The
starting shear and moment force are set to Vz = 1 MN and My = 1 MNm. The optimised
load factor λ is plotted in Figure 5.1 as a function of the number of elements nel. As
expected is it seen that the result converges.

along a cross section side.

5.1.2
Rectangular cross section
First a rectangular cross section is analysed. Diﬀerent stress distributions over the height
of the cross section can be assumed. In Figure 5.2 two diﬀerent plastic stress distributions
54
==PAGE:71==
5.1 Sub-model results with von Mises
purposed by [Vrouwenvelder, 2003] are illustrated.

(a)

Figure 5.2: Stress distributions in a rectangle
Analytical solutions for the relationship between the maximum value of the shear force V
and moment force M can obtain in combinations are derived in [Vrouwenvelder, 2003] for
the two diﬀerent stress distributions (a) and (b). The solutions in (a) and (b) are not real
lower bound solutions though, which Vrouwenvelder states, as equilibrium in the stress
state is not taken into account. [Vrouwenvelder, 2003] furthermore refers to a solution
that is derived by [Drucker, 1956], which includes this equilibrium in the derivation. This
solution is called (c). Analytical expressions for the three cases are:
(a):
M
Mp
+
 
V
Vp
!2
= 1
(b):
 
M
Mp
!2
+
 
V
Vp
!2
= 1
(c):
M
Mp
+
 
V
Vp
!4
= 1
[Drucker, 1956] states that a unique V M-curve for a speciﬁc cross section does not exist,
as it depends on the geometry and loading of the entire beam and not the properties of
the section alone. Drucker therefore investigates the V M relation for a rectangular cross
section in case of a cantilever beam with point load at the end. He investigates it by use
of the lower bound method, upper bound method and a local criterion.
For the lower bound method the point equilibria from (2.8) and (2.9) are used. Drucker
assumes that the normal stresses in the transverse direction are zero. Thereby the vertical
point equilibrium in (2.9) results in no variation in the shear stresses along the beam. As
a yield criteria he uses one close to von Mises, but not exactly the same. He assumes that
the maximum shear stress may not exceed fy/2 and uses the criterium:
σ2
x + 4τ 2
xs ≤f2
y
(5.2)
55
==PAGE:72==
Results and analysis
The number 4 in front of τxs is in von Mises, replaced by the number 3. Druckers yield
criterium makes the shear stress a bit more critical, thus a bit more conservative than von
Mises.
Drucker derives the lower bound solution by inserting the point equilibrium in the yield
criterion. He uses the boundary conditions for the cantilever beam, solves the diﬀerential
equation, and integrates the solution over the entire cross section. The solution in (c) is
not the exact lower bound as it is a ﬁtted curve, that Drucker makes, which is somewhere
in between his lower bound and upper bound solution. It is noted though, that it nearly
coincides with the lower bound.
With that in mind the curves from (a), (b) and (c) are plotted in Figure 5.3 together with
the numerical solution found by use of SubOpt, where a rectangular cross section build by
100 elements is used. The marks on the numerical curve illustrates the cases for which
the stress distributions are shown in Figure 5.4 and 5.5.

Comparison of the numerical curve with Druckers curve in (c) should be done with care.
In fact if Drucker had used von Mises yield criterion the curve might be shifted a little bit
upwards, making the curve a little less conservative. Furthermore Drucker assumes that
σs = 0 which from the vertical point equilibrium results in constant shear stress along the
beam. In the numerical model σs are also left out as they are assumed to be small, but
they are not assumed to be zero. Druckers assumption thus puts further constraint on his
analysis.
What can be said from Druckers curve though, is that it is not necessarily more conser-
vative then when using SubOpt. This depends on the shear to moment ratio. At high
shear Druckers curve is more conservative then the numerical, but at high moment it is
less conservative. It is also noted that using Druckers method on another type of beam
would give another solution. Druckers analysis thus indicates that a true lower bound
solution cannot be determined from a local analysis in the beam, but his analysis cannot
be directly compared to the numerical analysis.
In order to see how the normal force inﬂuences the result NV M-curves are illustrated

in Figure 5.6, together with the analytical curve (b). Np is the plastic capacity for pure
normal force. It is seen that the ﬁrst step from N/Np = 0 to N/Np = 0.2 does not inﬂuence
the curve as much as the step from N/Np = 0.4 to N/Np = 0.6. Furthermore it is noticed
that even when N/Np = 0.2, the numerical curve in some of its range lies above curve (b).

5.1.3
I-proﬁle
The example above was made for a rectangular cross section. [Vrouwenvelder, 2003] also
analyses an I-proﬁle, and it is of interest to compare with this as well. Vrouwenvelder
assumes that the shear force is carried by a uniform shear stress in the web and the
moment is carried by the whole cross section. In the ﬂanges the normal stresses from
the moment can obtain the yield stress fy and in the web the normal stress capacity
is reduced by the shear stress. The analytical relation between the shear and moment
capacity becomes:
M

where A is the whole cross section area and Aweb is the area of the web.

dimensions of the I-proﬁle inﬂuences the V M-curve.
It is chosen to use IPE 300 for the analysis. For the numerical analysis 50 elements for the
ﬂanges and 100 elements for the web are used. In Figure 5.7 NV M-diagram is illustrated
for the numerical solution in comparison with the analytical solution.

not inﬂuenced until approximately M/Mp = 0.75. This is explained below. It is further-

The stress distribution for curve (b) in Figure 5.2b reminds of the stress distribution for the
analytical curve in the I-proﬁle, except that the I-proﬁle can also obtain normal stresses in
its ﬂanges. As it is most optimal to use the ﬂanges for the stresses from the moment, the
ﬂanges are used for the moment until the point where the moment capacity in the ﬂanges
are reached before the shear capacity in the web. If the moment to shear ratio is larger
than this point, the web starts being used for the moment force as well and the shear force
capacity is being reduced. This is illustrated by showing the stress distribution for the
two marked points on the black curve. The ﬁrst point located where the moment does not
inﬂuence the shear capacity and the second point where the moment does inﬂuence the
shear capacity.
The stress distribution for the ﬁrst point is shown in Figure 5.8. It is here seen that there
is no normal stress in the web, and thereby the shear capacity in the web is not inﬂuenced.

5.2
Sub-model results with concrete yield condition
When the beam is made of reinforced concrete, the yield condition for a concrete disk is
used. This yield condition is more skew with respect to tension and compression than
the case for von Mises, and at some range of the yield surface, the shear stress capacity
increases with increasing compression. Optimising the stress distribution in a cross section
can therefore give some interesting results, where parts of the cross section obtain more
compression in order to obtain more shear. In this section sub-model 1 in SubOpt will be
used to illustrate this phenomenon.
For the analysis the following speciﬁcations are used:
fy =
500 MPa
fc =
40 MPa
ν =
1
Asx = Ass =
2000mm2/m
t =
0.2 m
=⇒ftx = fts=
5.0 MPa
=⇒Φx = Φs =
0.125
The reinforcement corresponds to approximately one Y16 per 100 mm. This lies within the
normal reinforced range for a disk. The yield condition for these speciﬁcation is illustrated
in Figure 5.11 and it can be seen how the shear stress capacity increases with increasing
compression stress. The crosses on the yield surface are the stress states on Figure 5.13.
More on that later.

5.2.2
Box cross section
To show some examples of how the stress distribution behaves for the concrete yield
condition when optimised upon, a box cross section will be used as example. Its geometry
for the center lines are 2 m x 1 m, and the material properties are set as written in the
top of this section, giving the yield surface on Figure 5.11.
In Figure 5.13 the optimised stress distribution for pure shear force is seen. It is observed
that even though there is only shear force acting on the cross section, there is not only
shear stresses, but also normal stresses in the cross section. The two webs are exposed to
compression stresses. Looking at the yield surface, these stresses allows for larger shear
stresses. As there is no normal force or moment at the cross section, the compression
stresses in the webs are balanced by tension stresses in the ﬂanges. The stress state in the
webs and in the ﬂanges are illustrated by the red and green cross respectively on the yield
surface in Figure 5.11 and it is seen that it is the tension stress in the ﬂanges which are
the limiting stresses.
What happens is that when imposing shear at the cross section, it is taken by an inclined
compression zone in the concrete and tension in the reinforcements. When reaching the
capacities in the reinforcement, no more shear can be taken unless more compression is
induced and this compression is balanced by activating the reinforcement in the ﬂanges.
If comparing with an analytical case of pure shear stresses in the webs and no normal
stresses, the maximum shear stress that it can obtain is 5 MPa. In this case, the limiting
shear force would become:
Vz = 5 MPa · 2 · 2 m · 0.2 m = 4.0 MN
With the optimised stress distribution in Figure 5.13 the limiting shear is 4.86 MN, which
is 22% higher than if there is no normal stress in the cross section.

Sub-model 2 including point equilibrium
Until now the results shown has been obtained from sub-model 1, where the point equi-
librium is not included. In this section the inﬂuence of the point equilibrium in sub-model
2 is investigated. The comparison is only made for concrete, but the idea should be the
same with von Mises.
5.3.1
Rectangular cross section
The thin vertical rectangular cross section with the dimension 2 m x 0.2 m is used. The
cross section has the same material properties as were used in the previous Section 5.2.
The cross section is investigated with the initial forces:
VL = 0.5MN
ML = −1 MNm
p = 0
This corresponds to the section forces at the ﬁxed end of a 2 m long cantilever beam, with
point load at the end. As the height of the beam is 2 m, this is a very high beam and it
should rather be regarded as a disk. This shows not to be of importance, when later all
ratios between shear force and moment are investigated in a V M-diagram.
Without point equilibrium
First results seen for the cross section is without the point equilibrium, found by using
sub-model 1.

sub-model 1.

Larger dx
In order to investigate the inﬂuence of the parameter dx, it is increased teen times to
dx = 0.1 m. The results are seen in Figure 5.18. With a larger piece of the beam the
results on the critical left side are still similar to the results without point equilibrium.
On the right side though, there are some bigger changes and a bit strange results are seen.
For the normal stresses it is clear that with larger dx, the change between the left and
right side must be larger. For the shear stresses, a bit strange behaviour is observed at
the right side. It is thereby seen that the left and critical side behaves exactly as without
equilibrium and the other side, which is not at its critical section forces, are more free to
distribute its stresses as long as it fulﬁls the equilibria set in the model.
The strange behaviour in the stresses on the right side, which is not critical, is not of
concern, as dx is relatively large and it makes more sense to keep it small as in Figure
5.17.

Changing critical side
With a change in the sign convention of ML from negative to positive, it is now the right
side that has the numerical largest section forces, because V is positive and M therefore
increases from left to right. It is on Figure 5.19 seen that the right side now has the
nice behaviour, while the left side complies in a way, so the equilibria are fulﬁlled. This
conﬁrms, that the critical side of the section obtains the most regular shape, while the
other side behaves more irregular, no matter which side is critical.

5.3.2
V M-diagram
Until now the analysis has shown that it makes no diﬀerent for the result to include the
point equilibrium. Only one case is analysed, and in order to see that the point equilibrium
does not have an inﬂuence no matter the V M relation, a V M-diagram is made for the
rectangular cross section with and without point equilibrium, seen in Figure 5.20.
69
==PAGE:86==
Results and analysis
M/Mp

0.2
0.4

0.3
0.4
0.5
0.6
0.7
0.8
0.9
1
without eq
with eq
Figure 5.20: VM-diagrams for rectangular concrete cross section
The two V M-curves lie on top of each other. Thus it can be concluded, that the point
equilibrium, as implemented in Section 3.5, has no inﬂuence on the solutions no matter
of the V M relation, which is because the most non-critical side is free to comply to the
equilibria. For a low V M relation - meaning low shear force compared to moment force
- the change in the moment along dx is very small. Even though the moment on the
non-critical side is close to the moment at the critical side, the stresses are free enough to
fulﬁl the equilibria.
This conclusion does not mean that the solutions are true lower bound solutions. Still the
normal stresses in the s-direction are not included and the vertical point equilibrium in
(2.9) is thus omitted. If the vertical equilibrium were included, it would mean that the
change in the shear stress in the x-direction would also be restricted by a change in σs
along s. Including this equilibrium would require a model that includes σs. How much this
would inﬂuence the result is diﬃcult to say. Therefore an analysis is made in Section 5.6,
where a program with a plastic disk is used to compare with the beam-model developed
in this thesis.
Furthermore it should be noted that the point equilibrium is in this analysis only used
locally at one small piece of the beam. Whether it would make a diﬀerence if it were
included continuously along the beam is discussed later.
5.4
Simple supported high beam
In this example the limit load for a high simple supported beam with distributed transverse
load is analysed.
This is done in order to compare the results from the beam-model
BeamOpt with an analytically known solution.
The analytical solution is found by using homogeneous stress ﬁelds. With this method
the optimal solution can be found by making the right distribution of the stress zones.
Comparing the results from the beam model with the analytical results ﬁrst of all will
show that the beam-model is capable of ﬁnding the right section force distribution along
70
==PAGE:87==
5.4 Simple supported high beam
the beam, and the optimal stress distribution in the critical section. Furthermore this
analysis is an investigation of how important the point equilibrium is and the inﬂuence of
the normal stress in the s-direction.
5.4.1
Analytical solution
Homogeneous stress ﬁelds are used as this method is a lower bound method, which can
ﬁnd the optimal solution for this simple supported case. The simple supported beam will
here be denoted as a disk. As the disk is symmetric around the center of the span, only
half the disk is analysed. The division of stress ﬁelds are seen in Figure 5.21. Whether the
failure of the disk happens from moment in the right side or shear in the left side depends
on the material parameter chosen for the concrete and reinforcement and the dimensions
of the disk. In this analysis two cases will be shown. One that results in moment failure
and one that results in shear failure.
